<p>Before emerging as a pop star, Madonna has spent her early years in <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">rock music</a> alongside her bands, Breakfast Club and Emmy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rock_star_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-rock_star-262"><span>[</span>262<span>]</span></a></sup> While performing with Emmy, Madonna recorded about 12-14 songs which resemble the <a href="/wiki/Punk_rock" title="Punk rock">punk rock</a> of that period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goldmine_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-goldmine-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> Her early rock roots also can be found on the demo album <i><a href="/wiki/Pre-Madonna" title="Pre-Madonna">Pre-Madonna</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rock_star_262-1"><a href="#cite_note-rock_star-262"><span>[</span>262<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Thomas_Erlewine" title="Stephen Thomas Erlewine">Stephen Thomas Erlewine</a> noted that with her self-titled debut album, Madonna began her career as a <a href="/wiki/Disco" title="Disco">disco</a> diva, in an era that did not have any such divas to speak of. In the beginning of the '80's, disco was an anathema to the mainstream pop, and according to Erlewine, Madonna had a huge role in popularizing <a href="/wiki/Dance_music" title="Dance music">dance music</a> as mainstream music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263"><span>[</span>263<span>]</span></a></sup> The album's songs reveal several key trends that have continued to define her success, including a strong dance-based idiom, catchy hooks, highly polished <a href="/wiki/Arrangement" title="Arrangement">arrangements</a> and Madonna's own vocal style. Her second album, <i>Like a Virgin</i> (1984), foreshadowed several trends in her later works. It contained references to classical works (<a href="/wiki/Pizzicato" title="Pizzicato">pizzicato</a> synthesizer line that opens "<a href="/wiki/Angel_(Madonna_song)" title="Angel (Madonna song)">Angel</a>"); potential negative reaction from social groups ("<a href="/wiki/Dress_You_Up" title="Dress You Up">Dress You Up</a>" was blacklisted by the <a href="/wiki/Parents_Music_Resource_Center" title="Parents Music Resource Center">Parents Music Resource Center</a>); and retro styles ("Shoo-Bee-Doo", Madonna's homage to <a href="/wiki/Motown" title="Motown">Motown</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fouzvocal_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-fouzvocal-264"><span>[</span>264<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Her mature artistic statement was visible in <i>True Blue</i> (1986) and <i>Like a Prayer</i> (1989). In <i>True Blue</i>, she incorporated <a href="/wiki/Classical_music" title="Classical music">classical music</a> in order to engage an older audience who had been skeptical of her music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265"><span>[</span>265<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Like a Prayer</i> introduced live recorded songs and incorporated different genres of music, including dance, <a href="/wiki/Funk" title="Funk">funk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_R%26B" title="Contemporary R&amp;B">R&amp;B</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gospel_music" title="Gospel music">gospel music</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bookalbum_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-bookalbum-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> Her versatility was further shown on <i>I'm Breathless</i>, which consists predominantly of the 1940s <a href="/wiki/Broadway_theatre" title="Broadway theatre">Broadway</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Showtune" title="Showtune">showtune</a>-flavoured <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swing_music" title="Swing music">swing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Big_band" title="Big band">big band</a> tracks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266"><span>[</span>266<span>]</span></a></sup> Madonna continued to compose ballads and <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_musical_terminology" title="Glossary of musical terminology">uptempo</a> dance songs for <i>Erotica</i> (1992) and <i>Bedtime Stories</i> (1994). Both albums explored element of <a href="/wiki/New_jack_swing" title="New jack swing">new jack swing</a>, with Jim Farber from <i>Entertainment Weekly</i> saying that "she could actually be viewed as new jack swing's godmother."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267"><span>[</span>267<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268"><span>[</span>268<span>]</span></a></sup> She tried to remain contemporary by incorporating samples, drum loops and <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop_music" title="Hip hop music">hip hop</a> into her music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269"><span>[</span>269<span>]</span></a></sup> With <i>Ray of Light</i>, Madonna brought <a href="/wiki/Electronic_music" title="Electronic music">electronic music</a> from its underground status into massive popularity in mainstream music scene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270"><span>[</span>270<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Madonna experimented with more <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">folk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acoustic_music" title="Acoustic music">acoustic music</a> in <i>Music</i> (2000) and <i>American Life</i> (2003).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271"><span>[</span>271<span>]</span></a></sup> A change was noted in the content of the songs in <i>Music</i>, with most of them being simple love songs, but with an underlying tone of melancholy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fouzvocal_264-1"><a href="#cite_note-fouzvocal-264"><span>[</span>264<span>]</span></a></sup> According to <i><a href="/wiki/Q_(magazine)" title="Q (magazine)">Q</a></i> magazine, <i>American Life</i> was characterized by "a thumping techno rhythm, liquid keyboard lines, an acoustic chorus and a bizarre Madonna rap."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Q_magazine_interview_May_2003_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-Q_magazine_interview_May_2003-272"><span>[</span>272<span>]</span></a></sup> The "conventional rock songs" of the album were suffused with dramatic lyrics about patriotism and composition, including the appearance of a gospel choir in the song "<a href="/wiki/Nothing_Fails" title="Nothing Fails">Nothing Fails</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Q_magazine_interview_May_2003_272-1"><a href="#cite_note-Q_magazine_interview_May_2003-272"><span>[</span>272<span>]</span></a></sup> Madonna returned to pure dance songs with <i>Confessions on a Dance Floor</i>, infusing club beats and retro music with the lyrics about <a href="/wiki/Paradox" title="Paradox">paradoxical</a> <a href="/wiki/Metaphor" title="Metaphor">metaphors</a> and reference to her earlier works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273"><span>[</span>273<span>]</span></a></sup> Madonna moved to urban direction with <i>Hard Candy</i> (2008), mixing R&amp;B and hip hop music with dance tunes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sischy2008_186-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sischy2008-186"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>MDNA</i> (2012) largely focused in electronic dance music, which she has embraced since <i>Ray of Light</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274"><span>[</span>274<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocal_style_and_instruments">Vocal style and instruments</span></h3>
<p>Possessing a <a href="/wiki/Mezzo-soprano" title="Mezzo-soprano">mezzo-soprano</a> vocal range,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275"><span>[</span>275<span>]</span></a></sup> Madonna has always been self-conscious about her voice, especially in comparison to her vocal idols such as <a href="/wiki/Ella_Fitzgerald" title="Ella Fitzgerald">Ella Fitzgerald</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prince_(musician)" title="Prince (musician)">Prince</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chaka_Khan" title="Chaka Khan">Chaka Khan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fouzvocal_264-2"><a href="#cite_note-fouzvocal-264"><span>[</span>264<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mark_Bego" title="Mark Bego">Mark Bego</a>, author of <i>Madonna: Blonde Ambition</i>, called her "the perfect vocalist for lighter-than-air songs", despite not being a "heavyweight talent."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276"><span>[</span>276<span>]</span></a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/MSNBC" title="MSNBC">MSNBC</a> critic Tony Sclafani, "Madonna's vocals are the key to her rock roots. Pop vocalists usually sing songs "straight," but Madonna employs subtext, irony, aggression and all sorts of vocal idiosyncrasies in the ways <a href="/wiki/John_Lennon" title="John Lennon">John Lennon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan" title="Bob Dylan">Bob Dylan</a> did."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rock_star_262-2"><a href="#cite_note-rock_star-262"><span>[</span>262<span>]</span></a></sup> Madonna used a bright, girlish vocal <a href="/wiki/Timbre" title="Timbre">timbre</a> in her early albums which became passé in her later works. The change was deliberate since she was constantly reminded of how the critics had once labelled her as "Minnie Mouse on helium".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fouzvocal_264-3"><a href="#cite_note-fouzvocal-264"><span>[</span>264<span>]</span></a></sup> During the filming of <i>Evita</i>, Madonna had to take vocal lessons, which increased her range further. Of this experience she commented, "I studied with a vocal coach for <i>Evita</i> and I realized there was a whole piece of my voice I wasn't using. Before, I just believed I had a really limited range and was going to make the most of it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277"><span>[</span>277<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Besides singing, Madonna has the ability to play several musical instruments. She learned to play drum and guitar from her then-boyfriend Dan Gilroy in the late 1970s before joining the Breakfast Club line-up as the drummer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gnojewski57_252-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gnojewski57-252"><span>[</span>252<span>]</span></a></sup> This helped her to form the band Emmy, where she performed as the guitarist and lead vocalist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gnojewski57_252-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gnojewski57-252"><span>[</span>252<span>]</span></a></sup> After her career breakthrough, Madonna focused mainly in singing but was also credited for playing <a href="/wiki/Cowbell_(instrument)" title="Cowbell (instrument)">cowbell</a> on <i>Madonna</i> (1983) and <a href="/wiki/Synthesizer" title="Synthesizer">synthesizer</a> on <i>Like a Prayer</i> (1989).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-music_credit_251-1"><a href="#cite_note-music_credit-251"><span>[</span>251<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1999, Madonna had studied for three months to play the <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violin</a> for the role as a violin teacher in the film <i>Music of the Heart</i>, before eventually leaving the project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278"><span>[</span>278<span>]</span></a></sup> After two decades, Madonna decided to perform with guitar again during the promotion of <i>Music</i> (2000). She took further lessons from guitarist <a href="/wiki/Monte_Pittman" title="Monte Pittman">Monte Pittman</a> to improve her guitar skill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279"><span>[</span>279<span>]</span></a></sup> Since then Madonna has played guitar on every tour, as well as her studio albums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-music_credit_251-2"><a href="#cite_note-music_credit-251"><span>[</span>251<span>]</span></a></sup> At the 2002 <a href="/wiki/Gibson_Guitar_Corporation" title="Gibson Guitar Corporation">Orville H. Gibson Guitar Awards</a>, she received nomination for Les Paul Horizon Award, which honors the most promising up-and-coming guitarist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280"><span>[</span>280<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influences">Influences</span></h3>
<p>According to Taraborrelli, the defining moment of Madonna's childhood was the tragic and untimely death of her beloved mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mom_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-mom-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> Psychiatrist Keith Ablow suggests her mother's death would have had an immeasurable impact on the young Madonna at a time when her personality was still forming. According to Ablow, the younger a child is at the time of a serious loss, the more profound the influence and the longer lasting the impact. He concludes that "some people never reconcile themselves to such a loss at an early age, Madonna is not different than them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mom_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-mom-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> Conversely, author <a href="/wiki/Lucy_O%27Brien" title="Lucy O'Brien">Lucy O'Brien</a> feels the impact of the rape she suffered is, in fact, the motivating factor behind everything Madonna has done, more important even than the death of her mother: "It's not so much grief at her mother's death that drives her, as the sense of abandonment that left her unprotected. She encountered her own worst possible scenario, becoming a victim of male violence, and thereafter turned that full-tilt into her work, reversing the equation at every opportunity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281"><span>[</span>281<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As they grew older, Madonna and her sisters would feel deep sadness as the vivid memory of their mother began drifting farther from them. They would study pictures of her and come to think that she resembled poet <a href="/wiki/Anne_Sexton" title="Anne Sexton">Anne Sexton</a> and Hollywood actresses. This would later raise Madonna's interest in poetry, with <a href="/wiki/Sylvia_Plath" title="Sylvia Plath">Sylvia Plath</a> being her favourite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mom_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-mom-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> Later, Madonna commented: "We were all wounded in one way or another by [her death], and then we spent the rest of our lives reacting to it or dealing with it or trying to turn into something else. The anguish of losing my mom left me with a certain kind of loneliness and an incredible longing for something. If I hadn't had that emptiness, I wouldn't have been so driven. Her death had a lot to do with me saying—after I got over my heartache—I'm going to be really strong if I can't have my mother. I'm going to take care of myself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mom_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-mom-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> Taraborrelli felt that in time, no doubt because of the devastation she felt, Madonna would never again allow herself, or even her daughter, to feel as abandoned as she had felt when her mother died. "Her death had taught [Madonna] a valuable lesson, that she would have to remain strong for herself because, she feared weakness—particularly her own—and wanted to be the queen of her own castle."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mom_3-8"><a href="#cite_note-mom-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marilyn_Monroe_in_Gentlemen_Prefer_Blondes_trailer.jpg"><img alt="Bust of a blond woman in short curled hair and wearing a bright pink, sleeveless dress. Putting both her hands up, she looks to the right of the image." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="479" data-file-width="678" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Marilyn_Monroe_in_Gentlemen_Prefer_Blondes_trailer.jpg/220px-Marilyn_Monroe_in_Gentlemen_Prefer_Blondes_trailer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Marilyn_Monroe_in_Gentlemen_Prefer_Blondes_trailer.jpg/330px-Marilyn_Monroe_in_Gentlemen_Prefer_Blondes_trailer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Marilyn_Monroe_in_Gentlemen_Prefer_Blondes_trailer.jpg/440px-Marilyn_Monroe_in_Gentlemen_Prefer_Blondes_trailer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marilyn_Monroe_in_Gentlemen_Prefer_Blondes_trailer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Marilyn_Monroe" title="Marilyn Monroe">Marilyn Monroe</a> <i>(pictured)</i> had a profound influence on Madonna</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1985, Madonna commented that the first song to ever make a strong impression on her was "<a href="/wiki/These_Boots_Are_Made_for_Walkin%27" title="These Boots Are Made for Walkin'">These Boots Are Made for Walkin'</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Sinatra" title="Nancy Sinatra">Nancy Sinatra</a>; she said it summed up her own "take-charge attitude".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hot_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-hot-282"><span>[</span>282<span>]</span></a></sup> As a young woman, she attempted to broaden her taste in literature, art, and music, and during this time became interested in classical music. She noted that her favorite style was <a href="/wiki/Baroque_music" title="Baroque music">baroque</a>, and loved <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Mozart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Chopin" title="Frédéric Chopin">Chopin</a> because she liked their "feminine quality".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283"><span>[</span>283<span>]</span></a></sup> Madonna's major influences include <a href="/wiki/Karen_Carpenter" title="Karen Carpenter">Karen Carpenter</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Supremes" title="The Supremes">The Supremes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">Led Zeppelin</a>, as well as dancers <a href="/wiki/Martha_Graham" title="Martha Graham">Martha Graham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Nureyev" title="Rudolf Nureyev">Rudolf Nureyev</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284"><span>[</span>284<span>]</span></a></sup> She also grew up listening to <a href="/wiki/David_Bowie" title="David Bowie">David Bowie</a>, whose show was the first rock concert she ever attended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285"><span>[</span>285<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Madonna's Italian-Catholic background and her relationship with her parents are reflected in the album <i>Like a Prayer</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-laprsreview_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-laprsreview-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> It was an evocation of the impact religion had on her career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286"><span>[</span>286<span>]</span></a></sup> Her video for the title track contains Catholic symbolism, such as the stigmata. During The Virgin Tour, she wore a <a href="/wiki/Rosary" title="Rosary">rosary</a> and prayed with it in the music video for "La Isla Bonita".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fouz_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-fouz-287"><span>[</span>287<span>]</span></a></sup> The "Open Your Heart" video sees her boss scolding her in the Italian language. On the Who's That Girl World Tour, she dedicated the song "Papa Don't Preach" to the Pope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fouz_287-1"><a href="#cite_note-fouz-287"><span>[</span>287<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288"><span>[</span>288<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>During her childhood, Madonna was inspired by actors, later saying, "I loved Carole Lombard and Judy Holliday and Marilyn Monroe. They were all incredibly funny ... and I saw myself in them ... my girlishness, my knowingness and my innocence."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hot_282-1"><a href="#cite_note-hot-282"><span>[</span>282<span>]</span></a></sup> Her "Material Girl" music video recreated Monroe's look in the song "<a href="/wiki/Diamonds_Are_a_Girl%27s_Best_Friend" title="Diamonds Are a Girl's Best Friend">Diamonds Are a Girl's Best Friend</a>", from the film <i><a href="/wiki/Gentlemen_Prefer_Blondes_(1953_film)" title="Gentlemen Prefer Blondes (1953 film)">Gentlemen Prefer Blondes</a></i> (1953). She studied the screwball comedies of the 1930s, particularly those of Lombard, in preparation for the film <i>Who's That Girl</i>. The video for "Express Yourself" (1989) was inspired by <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Lang" title="Fritz Lang">Fritz Lang</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Silent_film" title="Silent film">silent film</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Metropolis_(1927_film)" title="Metropolis (1927 film)">Metropolis</a></i> (1927). The video for "Vogue" recreated the style of <a href="/wiki/Glamour_(presentation)" title="Glamour (presentation)">Hollywood glamour</a> photographs, in particular those by <a href="/wiki/Horst_P._Horst" title="Horst P. Horst">Horst P. Horst</a>, and imitated the poses of <a href="/wiki/Marlene_Dietrich" title="Marlene Dietrich">Marlene Dietrich</a>, Carole Lombard, and <a href="/wiki/Rita_Hayworth" title="Rita Hayworth">Rita Hayworth</a>, while the lyrics referred to many of the stars who had inspired her, including <a href="/wiki/Bette_Davis" title="Bette Davis">Bette Davis</a>, described by Madonna as an idol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carrie_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-carrie-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289"><span>[</span>289<span>]</span></a></sup> However, Madonna's film career has been largely received negatively by the film critic community. Stephanie Zacharek, critic for <i>Time</i> magazine, stated that, "[Madonna] seems wooden and unnatural as an actress, and it's tough to watch, because she's clearly trying her damnedest."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-filmcareer_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-filmcareer-290"><span>[</span>290<span>]</span></a></sup> According to biographer <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Morton_(writer)" title="Andrew Morton (writer)">Andrew Morton</a>, "Madonna puts a brave face on the criticism, but privately she is deeply hurt."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-filmcareer_290-1"><a href="#cite_note-filmcareer-290"><span>[</span>290<span>]</span></a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Box_office_bomb" title="Box office bomb">box office bomb</a> <i>Swept Away</i> (2002), Madonna vowed that she would never again act in a film, hoping her repertoire as a bad actress would never be discussed again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-filmcareer_290-2"><a href="#cite_note-filmcareer-290"><span>[</span>290<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Influences also came to her from the art world, most notably through the works of Mexican artist <a href="/wiki/Frida_Kahlo" title="Frida Kahlo">Frida Kahlo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291"><span>[</span>291<span>]</span></a></sup> The music video of the song "Bedtime Story" featured images inspired by the paintings of Kahlo and <a href="/wiki/Remedios_Varo" title="Remedios Varo">Remedios Varo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292"><span>[</span>292<span>]</span></a></sup> Madonna is also a collector of <a href="/wiki/Tamara_de_Lempicka" title="Tamara de Lempicka">Tamara de Lempicka</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Art_Deco" title="Art Deco">Art Deco</a> paintings and has included them in her music videos and tours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293"><span>[</span>293<span>]</span></a></sup> Her video for "<a href="/wiki/Hollywood_(Madonna_song)" title="Hollywood (Madonna song)">Hollywood</a>" (2003) was an homage to the work of photographer <a href="/wiki/Guy_Bourdin" title="Guy Bourdin">Guy Bourdin</a>; Bourdin's son subsequently filed a lawsuit for unauthorised use of his father's work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294"><span>[</span>294<span>]</span></a></sup> Pop artist <a href="/wiki/Andy_Warhol" title="Andy Warhol">Andy Warhol</a>'s use of sadomasochistic imagery in his underground films were reflected in the music videos for "Erotica" and "Deeper and Deeper".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295"><span>[</span>295<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Madonna is dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Kabbalah" title="Kabbalah">Kabbalah</a>, and in 2004, she adopted the name <a href="/wiki/Esther_(given_name)" title="Esther (given name)">Esther</a> which in <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> means "star".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kab_296-0"><a href="#cite_note-kab-296"><span>[</span>296<span>]</span></a></sup> She has donated millions of dollars to New York and London schools teaching the subject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kab_296-1"><a href="#cite_note-kab-296"><span>[</span>296<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297"><span>[</span>297<span>]</span></a></sup> She faced opposition from rabbis who felt Madonna's adoption of the Kabbalah was sacrilegious and a case of celebrity <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/dilettantism" title="wikt:dilettantism">dilettantism</a>. Madonna defended her studies, saying: "It would be less controversial if I joined the Nazi Party", and that her involvement with the Kabbalah is "not hurting anybody".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298"><span>[</span>298<span>]</span></a></sup> The influence of the Kabbalah was subsequently observed in Madonna's music, especially albums like <i>Ray of Light</i> and <i>Music</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kab_296-2"><a href="#cite_note-kab-296"><span>[</span>296<span>]</span></a></sup> During the Re-Invention World Tour, at one point in the show, Madonna and her dancers wore T-shirts that read "Kabbalists Do It Better".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kab_296-3"><a href="#cite_note-kab-296"><span>[</span>296<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music_videos_and_performances">Music videos and performances</span></h3>
<p>In <i>The Madonna Companion</i>, biographers Allen Metz and Carol Benson noted that more than any other recent pop artist, Madonna had used MTV and music videos to establish her popularity and enhance her recorded work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metz2_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-metz2-299"><span>[</span>299<span>]</span></a></sup> According to them, many of her songs have the imagery of the music video in strong context, while referring to the music. <a href="/wiki/Cultural_critic" title="Cultural critic">Cultural critic</a> <a href="/wiki/Mark_C._Taylor" title="Mark C. Taylor">Mark C. Taylor</a> in his book <i>Nots</i> (1993) felt that the <a href="/wiki/Postmodern_art" title="Postmodern art">postmodern art</a> form par excellence is video and the reigning "queen of video" is Madonna. He further asserted that "the most remarkable creation of MTV is Madonna. The responses to Madonna's excessively provocative videos have been predictably contradictory."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300"><span>[</span>300<span>]</span></a></sup> The media and public reaction towards her most-discussed songs such as "Papa Don't Preach", "Like a Prayer", or "Justify My Love" had to do with the music videos created to promote the songs and their impact, rather than the songs themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metz2_299-1"><a href="#cite_note-metz2-299"><span>[</span>299<span>]</span></a></sup> Morton felt that "artistically, Madonna's songwriting is often overshadowed by her striking pop videos."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morton20_253-1"><a href="#cite_note-morton20-253"><span>[</span>253<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ExpressYourselfUnderGround_cropped.jpg"><img alt="A young blond woman dancing in a white brasierre" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="352" data-file-width="277" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/ExpressYourselfUnderGround_cropped.jpg/170px-ExpressYourselfUnderGround_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/ExpressYourselfUnderGround_cropped.jpg/255px-ExpressYourselfUnderGround_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/17/ExpressYourselfUnderGround_cropped.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ExpressYourselfUnderGround_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Madonna using the "Madonna mic" during the 1990 <a href="/wiki/Blond_Ambition_World_Tour" title="Blond Ambition World Tour">Blond Ambition World Tour</a>. She was one of the earliest adopters of hands-free headsets.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Madonna's initial music videos reflected her American and Hispanic mixed street style combined with a flamboyant glamor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metz2_299-2"><a href="#cite_note-metz2-299"><span>[</span>299<span>]</span></a></sup> She was able to transmit her avant-garde downtown New York fashion sense to the American audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301"><span>[</span>301<span>]</span></a></sup> The imagery and incorporation of Hispanic culture and Catholic symbolism continued with the music videos from the <i>True Blue</i> era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302"><span>[</span>302<span>]</span></a></sup> Author <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Kellner" title="Douglas Kellner">Douglas Kellner</a> noted, "such 'multiculturalism' and her culturally transgressive moves turned out to be highly successful moves that endeared her to large and varied youth audiences."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303"><span>[</span>303<span>]</span></a></sup> Madonna's Spanish look in the videos became the fashion trend of that time, in the form of boleros and layered skirts, accessorizing with rosary beads and a crucifix as in the video of "La Isla Bonita".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304"><span>[</span>304<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305"><span>[</span>305<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Academics noted that with her videos, Madonna was subtly reversing the usual role of male as the dominant sex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306"><span>[</span>306<span>]</span></a></sup> This symbolism and imagery was probably the most prevalent in the music video for "Like a Prayer". The video included scenes of an African-American church choir, Madonna being attracted to a statue of a black saint, and singing in front of burning crosses. This mix of the sacred and the profane upset the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Vatican</a> and resulted in the Pepsi commercial withdrawal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307"><span>[</span>307<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2003, MTV named her "The Greatest Music Video Star Ever" and said that "Madonna's innovation, creativity and contribution to the music video art form is what won her the award."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308"><span>[</span>308<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Madonna's emergence occurred during the advent of MTV; Chris Nelson from <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> spoke of pop artists like Madonna saying, "MTV, with its almost exclusively <a href="/wiki/Lip_sync" title="Lip sync">lip-synched</a> videos, ushered in an era in which average music fans might happily spend hours a day, every day, watching singers just mouth the words."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sync_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-sync-309"><span>[</span>309<span>]</span></a></sup> The symbiotic relationship between the music video and lip-syncing led to a desire for the spectacle and imagery of the music video to be transferred to live stage shows. He added, "Artists like Madonna and <a href="/wiki/Janet_Jackson" title="Janet Jackson">Janet Jackson</a> set new standards for showmanship, with concerts that included not only elaborate costumes and precision-timed pyrotechnics but also highly athletic dancing. These effects came at the expense of live singing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sync_309-1"><a href="#cite_note-sync-309"><span>[</span>309<span>]</span></a></sup> Thor Christensen of the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dallas_Morning_News" title="The Dallas Morning News">Dallas Morning News</a></i> commented that while Madonna earned a reputation for lip-syncing during her 1990 Blond Ambition World Tour, she has subsequently reorganized her performances by "stay[ing] mostly still during her toughest singing parts and [leaves] the dance routines to her backup troupe ... [r]ather than try to croon and dance up a storm at the same time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lip-sync_310-0"><a href="#cite_note-lip-sync-310"><span>[</span>310<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>To allow for greater movement while dancing and singing, Madonna was one of the earliest adopters of hands-free radio-frequency headset microphones, with the headset fastened over the ears or the top of the head, and the microphone capsule on a boom arm that extended to the mouth. Because of her prominent usage, the microphone design came to be known as the "Madonna mic".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311"><span>[</span>311<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312"><span>[</span>312<span>]</span></a></sup> Metz noted that Madonna represents a <a href="/wiki/Paradox" title="Paradox">paradox</a> as she is often perceived as living her whole life as a performance. While her big-screen performances are panned, her live performances are critical successes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313"><span>[</span>313<span>]</span></a></sup> Madonna was the first artist to have her concert tours as reenactment of her music videos. Author Elin Diamond explained that reciprocally, the fact that images from Madonna's videos can be recreated in a live setting enhances the realism of the original videos. Thus her live performances have become the means by which mediatized representations are naturalized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314"><span>[</span>314<span>]</span></a></sup> Taraborrelli said that encompassing multimedia, latest technology and sound systems, Madonna's concerts and live performances are deemed as "extravagant show piece, a walking art show."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315"><span>[</span>315<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_artists_influenced_by_Madonna" title="List of artists influenced by Madonna">List of artists influenced by Madonna</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Madonna_tribute_albums" title="List of Madonna tribute albums">List of Madonna tribute albums</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madonna_as_a_gay_icon" title="Madonna as a gay icon">Madonna as a gay icon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madonna_wannabe" title="Madonna wannabe">Madonna wannabe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madonna_Studies" title="Madonna Studies">Madonna Studies</a></div>
<p>Various music journalists, critical theorists, and authors have deemed Madonna the most influential female recording artist of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn2008_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-cnn2008-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316"><span>[</span>316<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317"><span>[</span>317<span>]</span></a></sup> Author Carol Clerk wrote that "during her career, Madonna has transcended the term 'pop star' to become a global cultural icon."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cultural_icon_two_318-0"><a href="#cite_note-cultural_icon_two-318"><span>[</span>318<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> of Spain wrote that "She became the first viral Master of Pop in history, years before the Internet was massively used. Madonna was everywhere; in the almighty music television channels, 'radio formulas', magazine covers and even in bookshops. A pop dialectic, never seen since <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a>'s reign, which allowed her to keep on the edge of tendency and commerciality."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319"><span>[</span>319<span>]</span></a></sup> Laura Barcella in her book <i>Madonna and Me: Women Writers on the Queen of Pop</i> (2012) wrote that "really, Madonna changed everything the musical landscape, the '80s look <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Du_jour" title="Du jour">du jour</a></i>, and most significantly, what a mainstream female pop star could (and couldn't) say, do, or accomplish in the public eye."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barcella_320-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barcella-320"><span>[</span>320<span>]</span></a></sup> William Langley from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">The Daily Telegraph</a></i> felt that "Madonna has changed the world's <a href="/wiki/Social_history" title="Social history">social history</a>, has done more things as more different people than anyone else is ever likely to."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321"><a href="#cite_note-321"><span>[</span>321<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alan_McGee" title="Alan McGee">Alan McGee</a> from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> felt that Madonna is a post-modern art, the likes of which we will never see again. He further asserted that Madonna and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Jackson" title="Michael Jackson">Michael Jackson</a> invented the terms <a href="/wiki/Honorific_nicknames_in_popular_music" title="Honorific nicknames in popular music">Queen and King of Pop</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322"><span>[</span>322<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Madonna_%C3%A0_Nice_8.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2283" data-file-width="1712" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Madonna_%C3%A0_Nice_8.jpg/170px-Madonna_%C3%A0_Nice_8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Madonna_%C3%A0_Nice_8.jpg/255px-Madonna_%C3%A0_Nice_8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Madonna_%C3%A0_Nice_8.jpg/340px-Madonna_%C3%A0_Nice_8.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Madonna_%C3%A0_Nice_8.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Madonna's use of religious symbolism during her performance on <a href="/wiki/The_MDNA_Tour" title="The MDNA Tour">The MDNA Tour</a> in 2012</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to Tony Sclafani from <a href="/wiki/MSNBC" title="MSNBC">MSNBC</a>, "It's worth noting that before Madonna, most music mega-stars were guy rockers; after her, almost all would be female singers ... When The Beatles hit America, they changed the paradigm of performer from solo act to band. Madonna changed it back—with an emphasis on the female."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323"><span>[</span>323<span>]</span></a></sup> Howard Kramer, curatorial director of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame_and_Museum" title="Rock and Roll Hall of Fame and Museum">Rock and Roll Hall of Fame and Museum</a>, asserted that "Madonna and the career she carved out for herself made possible virtually every other female pop singer to follow ... She certainly raised the standards of all of them ... She redefined what the parameters were for female performers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324"><span>[</span>324<span>]</span></a></sup> According to Fouz-Hernández, subsequent female singers such as <a href="/wiki/Britney_Spears" title="Britney Spears">Britney Spears</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christina_Aguilera" title="Christina Aguilera">Christina Aguilera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kylie_Minogue" title="Kylie Minogue">Kylie Minogue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spice_Girls" title="Spice Girls">Spice Girls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Destiny%27s_Child" title="Destiny's Child">Destiny's Child</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Lopez" title="Jennifer Lopez">Jennifer Lopez</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pink_(singer)" title="Pink (singer)">Pink</a> were like her "daughters in the very direct sense that they grew up listening to and admiring Madonna, and decided they wanted to be like her."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fouz4_325-0"><a href="#cite_note-fouz4-325"><span>[</span>325<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine included her in the list of the "25 Most Powerful Women of the Past Century", where she became one of only two singers to be included, alongside <a href="/wiki/Aretha_Franklin" title="Aretha Franklin">Aretha Franklin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326"><span>[</span>326<span>]</span></a></sup> She also topped <a href="/wiki/VH1" title="VH1">VH1</a>'s countdowns of "100 Greatest Women in Music" and "50 Greatest Women of the Video Era".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327"><span>[</span>327<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328"><span>[</span>328<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Madonna's use of sexual imagery has benefited her career and catalyzed public discourse on sexuality and feminism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fouz3_329-0"><a href="#cite_note-fouz3-329"><span>[</span>329<span>]</span></a></sup> As Roger Chapman documents in <i>Culture Wars: An Encyclopedia of Issues, Viewpoints, and Voices, Volume 1</i> (2010), she has drawn frequent condemnation from religious organizations, social conservatives and parental watchdog groups for her use of explicit, sexual imagery and lyrics, religious symbolism, and otherwise "irreverent" behavior in her live performances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330"><span>[</span>330<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>The Times</i> wrote that she had "started a revolution amongst women in music ... Her attitudes and opinions on sex, nudity, style and sexuality forced the public to sit up and take notice."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331"><span>[</span>331<span>]</span></a></sup> Professor <a href="/wiki/John_Fiske_(media_scholar)" title="John Fiske (media scholar)">John Fiske</a> noted that the sense of empowerment that Madonna offers is inextricably connected with the pleasure of exerting some control over the meanings of self, of sexuality, and of one's social relations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332"><span>[</span>332<span>]</span></a></sup> In <i>Doing Gender in Media, Art and Culture</i> (2009), the authors noted that Madonna, as a female celebrity, performer, and pop icon, is able to unsettle standing feminist reflections and debates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_der_Tuin_333-0"><a href="#cite_note-van_der_Tuin-333"><span>[</span>333<span>]</span></a></sup> According to lesbian feminist <a href="/wiki/Sheila_Jeffreys" title="Sheila Jeffreys">Sheila Jeffreys</a>, Madonna represents woman's occupancy of what <a href="/wiki/Monique_Wittig" title="Monique Wittig">Monique Wittig</a> calls the category of sex, as powerful, and appears to gleefully embrace the performance of the sexual corvée allotted to women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334"><span>[</span>334<span>]</span></a></sup> Professor <a href="/wiki/Sut_Jhally" title="Sut Jhally">Sut Jhally</a> has referred to Madonna as "an almost sacred feminist icon."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sut_Jhally_335-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sut_Jhally-335"><span>[</span>335<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Madonna has received acclaim as a role model for businesswomen in her industry, "achieving the kind of financial control that women had long fought for within the industry", and generating over $1.2 billion in sales within the first decade of her career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Routledge_336-0"><a href="#cite_note-Routledge-336"><span>[</span>336<span>]</span></a></sup> Professor Colin Barrow from <a href="/wiki/Cranfield_School_of_Management" title="Cranfield School of Management">Cranfield School of Management</a> described Madonna as "America's smartest businesswoman ... who has moved to the top of her industry and stayed there by constantly reinventing herself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337"><span>[</span>337<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/London_Business_School" title="London Business School">London Business School</a> academics called her a "dynamic entrepreneur" worth copying; they identified her vision of success, her understanding of the music industry, her ability to recognize her own performance limits (and thus bring in help), her willingness to work hard and her ability to adapt as the keys to her commercial success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LBS_338-0"><a href="#cite_note-LBS-338"><span>[</span>338<span>]</span></a></sup> Morton wrote that "Madonna is opportunistic, manipulative, and ruthless—somebody who won't stop until she gets what she wants—and that's something you can get at the expense of maybe losing your close ones. But that hardly mattered to her."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339"><span>[</span>339<span>]</span></a></sup> Hazel Blackmore and Rafael Fernández de Castro in the book <i>¿Qué es Estados Unidos?</i> from the <a href="/wiki/Fondo_de_Cultura_Econ%C3%B3mica" title="Fondo de Cultura Económica">Fondo de Cultura Económica</a>, noted: "Madonna has been undoubtedly the most important woman in the history of popular music and a great businesswoman in herself; creating fashion, breaking taboos and provoking controversies."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blackmore_340-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blackmore-340"><span>[</span>340<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Achievements">Achievements</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_awards_and_nominations_received_by_Madonna" title="List of awards and nominations received by Madonna">List of awards and nominations received by Madonna</a></div>
<p>Madonna has sold more than 300 million records worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341"><span>[</span>341<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342"><span>[</span>342<span>]</span></a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness World Records</a></i> acknowledged her as the best-selling female recording artist and <a href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_music_artists" title="List of best-selling music artists">the fourth best-selling act of all time</a>, behind The Beatles, Elvis Presley, and Michael Jackson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-343"><a href="#cite_note-343"><span>[</span>343<span>]</span></a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America" title="Recording Industry Association of America">Recording Industry Association of America</a> (RIAA), she is the best-selling female rock artist of the 20th century and the second <a href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_music_artists_in_the_United_States" title="List of best-selling music artists in the United States">top-selling female albums artist in the United States</a>, with 64.5 million <a href="/wiki/RIAA_certification" title="RIAA certification">certified</a> albums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-344"><a href="#cite_note-344"><span>[</span>344<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345"><span>[</span>345<span>]</span></a></sup> Madonna is the most certified artist of all time in United Kingdom, with 45 awards from the <a href="/wiki/British_Phonographic_Industry" title="British Phonographic Industry">British Phonographic Industry</a> (BPI) as of April 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-346"><a href="#cite_note-346"><span>[</span>346<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Billboard</i> named Madonna as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_highest-grossing_concert_tours" title="List of highest-grossing concert tours">top touring female artist of all time</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mdna_boxscore_229-1"><a href="#cite_note-mdna_boxscore-229"><span>[</span>229<span>]</span></a></sup> In May 2014, the magazine listed her as the fourth highest grossing touring act since 1990, with total earned revenue of $1.14 billion and 9.7 million attendance at her shows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-347"><a href="#cite_note-347"><span>[</span>347<span>]</span></a></sup> Madonna has been honored with 20 <a href="/wiki/MTV_Video_Music_Award" title="MTV Video Music Award">MTV Video Music Awards</a>—the most for any artist—including the lifetime achievement <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MTV_Video_Vanguard_Award" title="MTV Video Vanguard Award">Video Vanguard Award</a> in 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-348"><a href="#cite_note-348"><span>[</span>348<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Madonna holds the record for the most number-ones on all <a href="/wiki/Billboard_charts" title="Billboard charts"><i>Billboard</i> charts</a> with a total of 156 number-ones, including 12 on the <i>Billboard</i> Hot 100 and 43 on the <a href="/wiki/Hot_Dance_Club_Songs" title="Hot Dance Club Songs">Hot Dance Club Songs</a>. She has also scored 38 top-ten singles on the Hot 100, more than any other artist in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-349"><a href="#cite_note-349"><span>[</span>349<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2008, <i>Billboard</i> magazine ranked her at number two, behind <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a>, on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100_50th_Anniversary_Charts" title="Billboard Hot 100 50th Anniversary Charts"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100 All-Time Top Artists</a>, making her the most successful solo artist in the history of American singles chart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rsinducted_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-rsinducted-178"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Additional_charity_work">Additional charity work</span></h2>
<p>When Madonna found out in October 1986 that her mentor Martin Burgoyne was seriously ill with AIDS, she paid for his medical expenses at St. Vincent's Hospital, during a time when there was still not a great deal of information about the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-350"><a href="#cite_note-350"><span>[</span>350<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
